<p><b>Shamanism</b> (<span class="nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA_for_English" title="Help:IPA for English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ˈ/ primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="/ʃ/ 'sh' in 'shy'">ʃ</span><span title="/ɑː/ 'a' in 'father'">ɑː</span><span title="'m' in 'my'">m</span><span title="/ə/ 'a' in 'about'">ə</span><span title="'n' in 'no'">n</span></span>/</a></span></span> <span title="English pronunciation respelling"><a href="/wiki/Help:Pronunciation_respelling_key" title="Help:Pronunciation respelling key"><i><b><span class="smallcaps"><span style="font-variant: small-caps; text-transform: lowercase;">SHAH</span></span></b>-men</i></a></span> or <span class="nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA_for_English" title="Help:IPA for English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ˈ/ primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="/ʃ/ 'sh' in 'shy'">ʃ</span><span title="/eɪ/ long 'a' in 'base'">eɪ</span><span title="'m' in 'my'">m</span><span title="/ə/ 'a' in 'about'">ə</span><span title="'n' in 'no'">n</span></span>/</a></span></span> <span title="English pronunciation respelling"><a href="/wiki/Help:Pronunciation_respelling_key" title="Help:Pronunciation respelling key"><i><b><span class="smallcaps"><span style="font-variant: small-caps; text-transform: lowercase;">SHAY</span></span></b>-mən</i></a></span>) is a practice that involves a practitioner reaching <a href="/wiki/Altered_state_of_consciousness" title="Altered state of consciousness">altered states of consciousness</a> in order to perceive and interact with a spirit world and channel these transcendental energies into this world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">[1]</a></sup></p>
<p>A <b>shaman</b> is a person regarded as having access to, and influence in, the world of <a href="/wiki/Evocation" title="Evocation">benevolent and malevolent spirits</a>, who typically enters into a <a href="/wiki/Trance" title="Trance">trance state</a> during a <a href="/wiki/Ritual" title="Ritual">ritual</a>, and practices <a href="/wiki/Divination" title="Divination">divination</a> and <a href="/wiki/Healing" title="Healing">healing</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup> The word "shaman" probably originates from the Tungusic <a href="/wiki/Evenki_language" title="Evenki language">Evenki language</a> of <a href="/wiki/North_Asia" title="North Asia">North Asia</a>. According to ethnolinguist <a href="/wiki/Juha_Janhunen" title="Juha Janhunen">Juha Janhunen</a>, "the word is attested in all of the Tungusic idioms" such as Negidal, Lamut, Udehe/Orochi, Nanai, Ilcha, Orok, Manchu and Ulcha, and "nothing seems to contradict the assumption that the meaning 'shaman' also derives from Proto-Tungusic" and may have roots that extend back in time at least two millennia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup> The term was introduced to the west after <a href="/wiki/Russia" title="Russia">Russian</a> forces <a href="/wiki/Siege_of_Kazan" title="Siege of Kazan">conquered</a> the shamanistic <a href="/wiki/Khanate_of_Kazan" title="Khanate of Kazan">Khanate of Kazan</a> in 1552.</p>
<p>The term "shamanism" was first applied by western anthropologists as outside observers of the ancient religion of the <a href="/wiki/Turkic_peoples" title="Turkic peoples">Turks</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mongols" title="Mongols">Mongols</a>, as well as those of the neighboring <a href="/wiki/Tungusic_languages" title="Tungusic languages">Tungusic</a> and <a href="/wiki/Samoyedic_languages" title="Samoyedic languages">Samoyedic</a>-speaking peoples. Upon observing more religious traditions across the world, some <a class="mw-redirect mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Caucasian_people" title="Caucasian people">caucasian</a> anthropologists began to also use the term in a very broad sense, to describe unrelated magico-religious practices found within the <a href="/wiki/Ethnic_religion" title="Ethnic religion">ethnic religions</a> of other parts of Asia, Africa, Australasia and even completely unrelated parts of the Americas, as they believed these practices to be similar to one another.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Mircea_Eliade" title="Mircea Eliade">Mircea Eliade</a> writes, "A first definition of this complex phenomenon, and perhaps the least hazardous, will be: shamanism = 'technique of <a href="/wiki/Religious_ecstasy" title="Religious ecstasy">religious ecstasy</a>'."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Eli72_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-Eli72-5">[5]</a></sup> Shamanism encompasses the premise that shamans are intermediaries or messengers between the human world and the spirit worlds. Shamans are said to treat ailments/illness by mending the soul. Alleviating traumas affecting the soul/spirit restores the physical body of the individual to balance and wholeness. The shaman also enters <a href="/wiki/Otherworld" title="Otherworld">supernatural realms</a> or <a href="/wiki/Plane_(esotericism)" title="Plane (esotericism)">dimensions</a> to obtain solutions to problems afflicting the community. Shamans may visit other worlds/dimensions to bring guidance to misguided souls and to ameliorate illnesses of the human soul caused by foreign elements. The shaman operates primarily within the spiritual world, which in turn affects the human world. The restoration of balance results in the elimination of the ailment.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Eli72_5-1"><a href="#cite_note-Eli72-5">[5]</a></sup></p>
<p>Beliefs and practices that have been categorized this way as "shamanic" have attracted the interest of scholars from a wide variety of disciplines, including anthropologists, archaeologists, historians, religious studies scholars, philosophers, and psychologists. Hundreds of books and <a href="/wiki/Academic_publishing#Scholarly_paper" title="Academic publishing">academic papers</a> on the subject have been produced, with a peer-reviewed academic journal being devoted to the study of shamanism. In the 20th century, many westerners involved in the counter-cultural movement have created modern magico-religious practices influenced by their ideas of indigenous religions from across the world, creating what has been termed <a href="/wiki/Neoshamanism" title="Neoshamanism">neoshamanism</a> or the neoshamanic movement.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup> It has affected the development of many <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Neopaganism" title="Neopaganism">neopagan</a> practices, as well as faced a backlash and accusations of <a href="/wiki/Cultural_appropriation" title="Cultural appropriation">cultural appropriation</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Waveland_Press_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-Waveland_Press-7">[7]</a></sup> exploitation and misrepresentation when outside observers have tried to represent cultures they do not belong to.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wernitznig2_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-Wernitznig2-8">[8]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hobson_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hobson-9">[9]</a></sup></p>
<p></p>

<p></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Terminology">Terminology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Shamanism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Terminology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Etymology">Etymology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Shamanism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Etymology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Witsen%27s_Shaman.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="400" data-file-width="758" height="116" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/Witsen%27s_Shaman.JPG/220px-Witsen%27s_Shaman.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/Witsen%27s_Shaman.JPG/330px-Witsen%27s_Shaman.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/Witsen%27s_Shaman.JPG/440px-Witsen%27s_Shaman.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Witsen%27s_Shaman.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The earliest known depiction of a Siberian shaman, drawn by the Dutch explorer <a href="/wiki/Nicolaes_Witsen" title="Nicolaes Witsen">Nicolaes Witsen</a>, who wrote an account of his travels among Samoyedic- and Tungusic-speaking peoples in 1692. Witsen labelled the illustration as a "Priest of the Devil," giving this figure clawed feet to express what he thought were demonic qualities.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The word "shaman" probably originates from the <a href="/wiki/Evenki_language" title="Evenki language">Evenki</a> word "<i>šamán,</i>" most likely from the southwestern dialect spoken by the Sym Evenki peoples.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup> The Tungusic term was subsequently adopted by Russians interacting with the indigenous peoples in Siberia. It is found in the memoirs of the exiled Russian churchman <a href="/wiki/Avvakum" title="Avvakum">Avvakum</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup></p>
<p>The word was brought to Western Europe in the late 17th century by the Dutch traveler <a href="/wiki/Nicolaes_Witsen" title="Nicolaes Witsen">Nicolaes Witsen</a>, who reported his stay and journeys among the Tungusic and Samoyedic-speaking indigenous peoples of Siberia in his book <i>Noord en Oost Tataryen</i> (1692).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Adam_Brand_(explorer)" title="Adam Brand (explorer)">Adam Brand</a>, a merchant from Lübeck, published in 1698 his account of a Russian embassy to China; a translation of his book, published the same year, introduced the word <i>shaman</i> to English speakers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup></p>
<p>The etymology of the Evenki word is sometimes connected to a Tungus root <i>ša-</i> "to know".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cogmap_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-cogmap-15">[15]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Di.C3.B3szegi_1962:13_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-Di.C3.B3szegi_1962:13-16">[16]</a></sup> This has been questioned on linguistic grounds: "The possibility cannot be completely rejected, but neither should it be accepted without reservation since the assumed derivational relationship is phonologically irregular (note especially the vowel quantities)."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup> Other scholars assert that the word comes directly from the <a href="/wiki/Manchu_language" title="Manchu language">Manchu language</a>, and as such would be the only commonly used English word that is a loan from this language.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup></p>
<p>However, <a href="/wiki/Mircea_Eliade" title="Mircea Eliade">Mircea Eliade</a> noted that the <a href="/wiki/Sanskrit" title="Sanskrit">Sanskrit</a> word <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sramana" title="Sramana">śramaṇa</a>,</i> designating a wandering monastic or holy figure, has spread to many Central Asian languages along with <a href="/wiki/Buddhism" title="Buddhism">Buddhism</a> and could be the ultimate origin of the Tungusic word.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup> This proposal has been thoroughly critiqued since 1917. Ethnolinguist Juha Janhunen regards it as an "anachronism" and an "impossibility" that is nothing more than a "far-fetched etymology."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup></p>
<p>21st-century anthropologist and archeologist Silvia Tomaskova argues that by the mid-1600s, many Europeans applied the Arabic term <i>shaitan,</i> meaning "devil," to the non-Christian practices and beliefs of indigenous peoples beyond the Ural Mountains.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup> She suggests that <i>shaman</i> may have entered the various Tungus dialects as a corruption of this term, and then been told to the white, male and Christian missionaries, explorers, soldiers and colonial administrators with whom the people had increasing contact for centuries. Ethnolinguists did not develop as a discipline, nor reach them, until the late 19th century, and may have mistakenly "read backward" in time for the origin of this word.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Definitions">Definitions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Shamanism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Definitions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:SB_-_Altay_shaman_with_drum.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="607" data-file-width="395" height="338" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/SB_-_Altay_shaman_with_drum.jpg/220px-SB_-_Altay_shaman_with_drum.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/SB_-_Altay_shaman_with_drum.jpg/330px-SB_-_Altay_shaman_with_drum.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/2/2d/SB_-_Altay_shaman_with_drum.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:SB_-_Altay_shaman_with_drum.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Russian postcard based on a photo taken in 1908 by S.I. Borisov, showing a female shaman, of probable <a href="/wiki/Khakas_people" title="Khakas people">Khakas</a> ethnicity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>There is no single agreed-upon definition for the word "shamanism" among anthropologists. The English historian <a href="/wiki/Ronald_Hutton" title="Ronald Hutton">Ronald Hutton</a> noted that by the dawn of the 21st century, there were four separate definitions of the term which appeared to be in use. The first of these uses the term to refer to "anybody who contacts a spirit world while in an altered state of consciousness." The second definition limits the term to refer to those who contact a spirit world while in an altered state of consciousness at the behest of others. The third definition attempts to distinguish shamans from other magico-religious specialists who are believed to contact spirits, such as "<a href="/wiki/Mediumship" title="Mediumship">mediums</a>", "<a href="/wiki/Witch_doctor" title="Witch doctor">witch doctors</a>", "spiritual healers" or "prophets," by claiming that shamans undertake some particular technique not used by the others. Problematically, scholars advocating the third view have failed to agree on what the defining technique should be. The fourth definition identified by Hutton uses "shamanism" to refer to the indigenous religions of Siberia and neighboring parts of Asia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup> According to the Golomt Center for Shamanic Studies, a Mongolian organisation of shamans, the Evenk word <i>shaman</i> would more accurately be translated as "priest".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Initiation_and_learning">Initiation and learning</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Shamanism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Initiation and learning">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Shamans are normally "called" by dreams or signs which require lengthy training. However, shamanic powers may be "inherited".</p>
<p>Turner and colleagues<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup> mention a phenomenon called shamanistic initiatory crisis, a <a href="/wiki/Rite_of_passage" title="Rite of passage">rite of passage</a> for shamans-to-be, commonly involving physical illness and/or psychological crisis. The significant role of initiatory illnesses in the calling of a shaman can be found in the detailed case history of <a href="/wiki/Oroqen_people" title="Oroqen people">Chuonnasuan</a>, the last master shaman among the Tungus peoples in Northeast China.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup></p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Wounded_healer" title="Wounded healer">wounded healer</a> is an <a href="/wiki/Archetype" title="Archetype">archetype</a> for a shamanic trial and journey. This process is important to the young shaman. S/he undergoes a type of sickness that pushes her or him to the brink of death. This happens for two reasons:</p>
<ol>
<li>The shaman crosses over to the underworld. This happens so the shaman can venture to its depths to bring back vital information for the sick, and the tribe.</li>
<li>The shaman must become sick to understand sickness. When the shaman overcomes his or her own sickness, s/he will hold the cure to heal all that suffer. This is the uncanny mark of the wounded healer.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup></li>
</ol>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Roles">Roles</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Shamanism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Roles">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:COLLECTIE_TROPENMUSEUM_Een_shamaan_op_Zuid-Boeroe_bezweert_boze_geesten_de_kinderen_te_verlaten_waarbij_hij_een_geldstuk_en_een_sirihnoot_offert_TMnr_10001031.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="499" data-file-width="700" height="157" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/COLLECTIE_TROPENMUSEUM_Een_shamaan_op_Zuid-Boeroe_bezweert_boze_geesten_de_kinderen_te_verlaten_waarbij_hij_een_geldstuk_en_een_sirihnoot_offert_TMnr_10001031.jpg/220px-thumbnail.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/COLLECTIE_TROPENMUSEUM_Een_shamaan_op_Zuid-Boeroe_bezweert_boze_geesten_de_kinderen_te_verlaten_waarbij_hij_een_geldstuk_en_een_sirihnoot_offert_TMnr_10001031.jpg/330px-thumbnail.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/COLLECTIE_TROPENMUSEUM_Een_shamaan_op_Zuid-Boeroe_bezweert_boze_geesten_de_kinderen_te_verlaten_waarbij_hij_een_geldstuk_en_een_sirihnoot_offert_TMnr_10001031.jpg/440px-thumbnail.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>

<td style="padding:0.3em 0.4em 0.3em;font-weight:bold;border-top: 1px solid #aaa; border-bottom: 1px solid #aaa;padding-top:0.15em;"><a href="/wiki/Social_anthropology" title="Social anthropology">Social</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cultural_anthropology" title="Cultural anthropology">cultural anthropology</a></td>

<tr>
<td style="text-align:right;font-size:115%;padding-top: 0.6em;">
<div class="plainlinks hlist navbar mini">
<ul>
<li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Anthropology_of_religion" title="Template:Anthropology of religion"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li>
<li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Anthropology_of_religion" title="Template talk:Anthropology of religion"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li>
<li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Anthropology_of_religion&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li>

<p>Shamans claim to gain knowledge and the power to heal by entering into the <a href="/wiki/Spirit_world_(Spiritualism)" title="Spirit world (Spiritualism)">spiritual world</a> or dimension. Most shamans have <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dreams" title="Dreams">dreams</a> or <a href="/wiki/Vision_(spirituality)" title="Vision (spirituality)">visions</a> that convey certain messages. The shaman may have or acquire many <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Spirit_guides" title="Spirit guides">spirit guides</a>, who often guide and direct the shaman in his/her travels in the spirit world. These spirit guides are always present within the shaman, although others encounter them only when the shaman is in a trance. The spirit guide energizes the shaman, enabling him/her to enter the spiritual dimension. The shaman heals within the spiritual dimension by returning 'lost' parts of the human soul from wherever they have gone. The shaman also cleanses excess negative energies, which confuse or pollute the soul.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2009)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>Shamans act as <a href="/wiki/Mediation" title="Mediation">mediators</a> in their culture.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mediator_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-mediator-28">[28]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mediator2_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-mediator2-29">[29]</a></sup> The shaman communicates with the spirits on behalf of the community, including the spirits of the deceased. The shaman communicates with both living and dead to alleviate unrest, unsettled issues, and to deliver gifts to the spirits.</p>
<p>Among the <a href="/wiki/Selkup_people" title="Selkup people">Selkups</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Merginae" title="Merginae">sea duck</a> is a spirit animal. Ducks fly in the air and dive in the water. Thus ducks are believed to belong to both the upper world and the world below.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hoppal_a_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hoppal_a-30">[30]</a></sup> Among other Siberian peoples, these characteristics are attributed to water fowl in general.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup> The upper world is the afterlife primarily associated with deceased humans and is believed to be accessed by soul journeying through a portal in the sky. The lower world or "world below" is the afterlife primarily associated with animals and is believed to be accessed by soul journeying through a portal in the earth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup> In shamanic cultures many animals are regarded as spirit animals.</p>
<p>Shamans perform a variety of functions depending upon their respective cultures;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-multfunc_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-multfunc-33">[33]</a></sup> healing,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sem-shamheal_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-sem-shamheal-34">[34]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup> leading a <a href="/wiki/Sacrifice" title="Sacrifice">sacrifice</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup> preserving the <a href="/wiki/Tradition" title="Tradition">tradition</a> by <a href="/wiki/Storytelling" title="Storytelling">storytelling</a> and songs,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-memory_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-memory-37">[37]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Fortune-telling" title="Fortune-telling">fortune-telling</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup> and acting as a <a href="/wiki/Psychopomp" title="Psychopomp">psychopomp</a> (literal meaning, "guide of souls").<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-psychopomp_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-psychopomp-39">[39]</a></sup> A single shaman may fulfill several of these functions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-multfunc_33-1"><a href="#cite_note-multfunc-33">[33]</a></sup></p>
<p>The functions of a shaman may include either guiding to their proper abode the souls of the dead (which may be guided either one-at-a-time or in a cumulative group, depending on culture), and/or curing (healing) of ailments. The ailments may be either purely physical afflictions—such as disease, which may be cured by gifting, flattering, threatening, or wrestling the disease-spirit (sometimes trying all these, sequentially), and which may be completed by displaying a supposedly extracted token of the disease-spirit (displaying this, even if "fraudulent", is supposed to impress the disease-spirit that it has been, or is in the process of being, defeated, so that it will retreat and stay out of the patient's body), or else mental (including psychosomatic) afflictions—such as persistent terror (on account of a frightening experience), which may be likewise cured by similar methods. In most languages a different term other than the one translated "shaman" is usually applied to a religious official leading sacrificial rites ("priest"), or to a raconteur ("sage") of traditional lore; there may be more of an overlap in functions (with that of a shaman), however, in the case of an interpreter of omens or of dreams.</p>
<p>There are distinct types of shaman who perform more specialized functions. For example, among the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nani_people" title="Nani people">Nani people</a>, a distinct kind of shaman acts as a <a href="/wiki/Psychopomp" title="Psychopomp">psychopomp</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup> Other specialized shamans may be distinguished according to the type of spirits, or realms of the spirit world, with which the shaman most commonly interacts. These roles vary among the <a href="/wiki/Nenets_people" title="Nenets people">Nenets</a>, <a href="/wiki/Enets_people" title="Enets people">Enets</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Selkup_people" title="Selkup people">Selkup</a> shaman.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup> Among the <a href="/wiki/Huichol_people" title="Huichol people">Huichol</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mexico_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mexico-43">[43]</a></sup> there are two categories of shaman. This demonstrates the differences among shamans within a single tribe.</p>
<p>Among the <a href="/wiki/Hmong_people" title="Hmong people">Hmong people</a>, the shaman or the <i>Ntxiv Neej</i> (Tee-Neng), acts as healer. The Ntxiv Neej also performs rituals/ceremonies (soul retrievals) designed to call the soul back from its many travels to the physical human body. A Ntxiv Neej may use several shamanistic tools such as swords, divinity horns, a gong (drum), or finger bells/jingles. All tools serve to protect the spirits from the eyes of the unknown, thus enabling the Ntxiv Neej to deliver souls back to their proper owner. The Ntxiv Neej may wear a white, red, or black veil to disguise the soul from its attackers in the spiritual dimension.</p>
<p>Boundaries between the shaman and laity are not always clearly defined. Among the <a href="/wiki/Barasana" title="Barasana">Barasana</a> of Brazil, there is no absolute difference between those recognized as shamans and those who are not.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (May 2012)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> At the lowest level, most adults have abilities as shamans and will carry out the same functions as those who have a widespread reputation for their powers and knowledge. The Barasana shaman knows more <a href="/wiki/Mythology" title="Mythology">myths</a> and understands their meaning better, nonetheless the majority of adults also know many myths.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-barasana-myths_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-barasana-myths-44">[44]</a></sup></p>
<p>Among <a href="/wiki/Inuit" title="Inuit">Inuit</a> peoples the laity have experiences which are commonly attributed to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shamanism_among_Eskimo_peoples#Shamanism_in_various_Eskimo_groups" title="Shamanism among Eskimo peoples">shamans of those Inuit groups</a>. <a href="/wiki/Daydream" title="Daydream">Daydream</a>, reverie, and <a href="/wiki/Trance" title="Trance">trance</a> are not restricted to shamans.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mer-BecHalfHid_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mer-BecHalfHid-45">[45]</a></sup> Control over / alliance with helping spirits is the primary characteristic attributed to shamans. The laity usually employ <a href="/wiki/Amulet" title="Amulet">amulets</a>, spells, formulas, songs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mer-BecHalfHid_45-1"><a href="#cite_note-Mer-BecHalfHid-45">[45]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup> Among the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Greenland_Inuit" title="Greenland Inuit">Greenland Inuit</a>, the laity have greater capacity to relate with spiritual beings. These people are often apprentice shamans who failed to complete their initiations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-failed-shaman_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-failed-shaman-47">[47]</a></sup></p>
<p>The assistant of an <a href="/wiki/Oroqen_people" title="Oroqen people">Oroqen</a> shaman (called <i>jardalanin</i>, or "second spirit") knows many things about the associated beliefs. He or she accompanies the rituals and interprets the behavior of the shaman.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Guan_Kouni_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-Guan_Kouni-48">[48]</a></sup> Despite these functions, the jardalanin is <i>not</i> a shaman. For this interpretative assistant, it would be unwelcome to fall into trance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-interpreter_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-interpreter-49">[49]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Ecological_aspect">Ecological aspect</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Shamanism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Ecological aspect">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Resources for human consumption are easily <a href="/wiki/Tropical_rainforest#Habitation" title="Tropical rainforest">depletable in tropical rainforests</a>. Among the <a href="/wiki/Tucano_people" title="Tucano people">Tucano people</a>, a sophisticated system exists for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Environmental_resources_management" title="Environmental resources management">environmental resources management</a> and for avoiding resource depletion through overhunting. This system is conceptualized mythologically and symbolically by the belief that breaking hunting restrictions may cause illness. As the primary teacher of tribal symbolism, the shaman may have a leading role in this <a href="/wiki/Ecology" title="Ecology">ecological</a> management, actively restricting hunting and fishing. The shaman is able to "release" game animals, or their souls, from their hidden abodes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-eco_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-eco-50">[50]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Piaroa_people" title="Piaroa people">Piaroa people</a> have ecological concerns related to shamanism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ecopia_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-ecopia-52">[52]</a></sup> Among the <a href="/wiki/Inuit" title="Inuit">Inuit</a>, shamans fetch the souls of game from remote places,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup> or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Soul_travel" title="Soul travel">soul travel</a> to ask for game from mythological beings like the <a href="/wiki/Sedna_(mythology)" title="Sedna (mythology)">Sea Woman</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Economics">Economics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Shamanism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Economics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The way shamans get sustenance and take part in everyday life varies across cultures. In many Inuit groups, they provide services for the community and get a "due payment" (cultures),<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Words_to_watch#Unsupported_attributions" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Words to watch"><span title="The material near this tag possibly uses too-vague attribution or weasel words. (February 2012)">who?</span></a></i>]</sup> believe the payment is given to the helping spirits<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mshare_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-mshare-56">[56]</a></sup> but these goods are only "welcome addenda." They are not enough to enable shamanizing as a full-time activity. Shamans live like any other member of the group, as a hunter or housewife. Due to the popularity of <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Ayahuasca_tourism&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Ayahuasca tourism (page does not exist)">ayahuasca tourism</a> in South America, there are practitioners in areas frequented by backpackers who make a living from leading ceremonies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-failed-shaman_47-1"><a href="#cite_note-failed-shaman-47">[47]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mshare_56-1"><a href="#cite_note-mshare-56">[56]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Beliefs">Beliefs</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Shamanism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Beliefs">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>There are many variations of shamanism throughout the world, but several common beliefs are shared by all forms of shamanism. Common beliefs identified by <a href="/wiki/Mircea_Eliade" title="Mircea Eliade">Eliade</a> (1972)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Eli72_5-2"><a href="#cite_note-Eli72-5">[5]</a></sup> are the following:</p>
<ul>
<li>Spirits exist and they play important roles both in individual lives and in human society.</li>
<li>The shaman can communicate with the spirit world.</li>
<li>Spirits can be benevolent or malevolent.</li>
<li>The shaman can treat sickness caused by malevolent spirits.</li>
<li>The shaman can employ <a href="/wiki/Trance" title="Trance">trance</a> inducing techniques to incite visionary ecstasy and go on <a href="/wiki/Vision_quest" title="Vision quest">vision quests</a>.</li>
<li>The shaman's spirit can leave the body to enter the <a href="/wiki/Supernatural" title="Supernatural">supernatural</a> world to search for answers.</li>
<li>The shaman evokes animal images as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Spirit_guides" title="Spirit guides">spirit guides</a>, <a href="/wiki/Omen" title="Omen">omens</a>, and message-bearers.</li>
<li>The shaman can perform other varied forms of <a href="/wiki/Divination" title="Divination">divination</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Scry" title="Scry">scry</a>, throw bones/<a href="/wiki/Runes" title="Runes">runes</a>, and sometimes foretell of future events.</li>
</ul>
<p>Shamanism is based on the premise that the visible world is pervaded by invisible forces or spirits which affect the lives of the living.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Peru_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-Peru-57">[57]</a></sup> Although the causes of disease lie in the spiritual realm, inspired by malicious spirits, both spiritual and physical methods are used to heal. Commonly, a shaman "enters the body" of the patient to confront the spiritual infirmity and heals by banishing the infectious spirit.</p>
<p>Many shamans have expert knowledge of medicinal plants native to their area, and an herbal treatment is often prescribed. In many places shamans learn directly from the plants, harnessing their effects and healing properties, after obtaining permission from the indwelling or patron spirits. In the Peruvian Amazon Basin, shamans and <i><a href="/wiki/Curandero" title="Curandero">curanderos</a></i> use medicine songs called <i><a href="/wiki/Icaro" title="Icaro">icaros</a></i> to evoke spirits. Before a spirit can be summoned it must teach the shaman its song.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Peru_57-1"><a href="#cite_note-Peru-57">[57]</a></sup> The use of <a href="/wiki/Totem" title="Totem">totemic</a> items such as rocks with special powers and an <a href="/wiki/Animism" title="Animism">animating spirit</a> is common.</p>
<p>Such practices are presumably very ancient. <a href="/wiki/Plato" title="Plato">Plato</a> wrote in his <i><a href="/wiki/Phaedrus_(dialogue)" title="Phaedrus (dialogue)">Phaedrus</a></i> that the "first prophecies were the words of an oak", and that those who lived at that time found it rewarding enough to "listen to an oak or a stone, so long as it was telling the truth".</p>
<p>Belief in witchcraft and sorcery, known as <i><a href="/wiki/Brujer%C3%ADa" title="Brujería">brujería</a></i> in Latin America, exists in many societies. Other societies assert all shamans have the power to both cure and kill. Those with shamanic knowledge usually enjoy great power and prestige in the community,<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2009)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> but they may also be regarded suspiciously or fearfully as potentially harmful to others.</p>
<p>By engaging in their work, a shaman is exposed to significant personal risk, from the spirit world, from enemy shamans, or from the means employed to alter the shaman's <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/State_of_consciousness" title="State of consciousness">state of consciousness</a>. Shamanic plant materials can be toxic or fatal if misused. Failure to return from an <a href="/wiki/Astral_projection" title="Astral projection">out-of-body journey</a> can lead to death.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (May 2012)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> <a href="/wiki/Spell_(paranormal)" title="Spell (paranormal)">Spells</a> are commonly used to protect against these dangers, and the use of more dangerous plants is often very highly ritualized.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Soul_and_spirit_concepts">Soul and spirit concepts</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Shamanism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Soul and spirit concepts">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The variety of functions described above may seem like distinct tasks, but they may be united by underlying soul and spirit concepts.</p>
<dl>
<dt><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Soul_(spirit)" title="Soul (spirit)">Soul</a></dt>
<dd>This concept can generally explain more, seemingly unassociated phenomena in shamanism:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-all-soul_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-all-soul-58">[58]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-soulsham_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-soulsham-60">[60]</a></sup></dd>
</dl>
<dl>
<dt><a href="/wiki/Healing" title="Healing">Healing</a></dt>
<dd>This concept may be based closely on the soul concepts of the belief system of the people served by the shaman.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sem-shamheal_34-1"><a href="#cite_note-sem-shamheal-34">[34]</a></sup> It may consist of retrieving the lost soul of the ill person.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-resoul_61-0"><a href="#cite_note-resoul-61">[61]</a></sup> See also the <a href="/wiki/Soul_dualism" title="Soul dualism">soul dualism</a> concept.</dd>
</dl>
<dl>
<dt><a href="/wiki/Scarcity" title="Scarcity">Scarcity</a> of hunted game</dt>
<dd>This problem can be solved by "releasing" the souls of the animals from their hidden abodes. Besides that, many <a href="/wiki/Taboo" title="Taboo">taboos</a> may prescribe the behavior of people towards game, so that the souls of the animals do not feel angry or hurt, or the pleased soul of the already killed prey can tell the other, still living animals, that they can allow themselves to be caught and killed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gab-KarEszk_63-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gab-KarEszk-63">[63]</a></sup> For the ecological aspects of shamanistic practice, and related beliefs, see below.</dd>
</dl>
<dl>
<dt><a href="/wiki/Infertility" title="Infertility">Infertility</a> of women</dt>
<dd>This problem can be cured by obtaining the soul of the expected child.</dd>
</dl>
<dl>
<dt><a href="/wiki/Spirit" title="Spirit">Spirits</a></dt>
<dd>Beliefs related to <a href="/wiki/Spirit" title="Spirit">spirits</a> can explain many different phenomena.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup> For example, the importance of <a href="/wiki/Storytelling" title="Storytelling">storytelling</a>, or acting as a singer, can be understood better if we examine the whole belief system. A person who can memorize long texts or songs, and play an instrument, may be regarded as the beneficiary of contact with the spirits (e.g. <a href="/wiki/Khanty_people" title="Khanty people">Khanty people</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-singtellplay_65-0"><a href="#cite_note-singtellplay-65">[65]</a></sup></dd>
</dl>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Practice">Practice</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Shamanism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Practice">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Religious_ecstasy" title="Religious ecstasy">Religious ecstasy</a></div>
<p>Generally, the shaman traverses the <a href="/wiki/Axis_mundi" title="Axis mundi">axis mundi</a> and enters the spirit world by effecting a transition of consciousness, entering into an <a href="/wiki/Religious_ecstasy" title="Religious ecstasy">ecstatic</a> <a href="/wiki/Trance" title="Trance">trance</a>, either <a href="/wiki/Autosuggestion" title="Autosuggestion">autohypnotically</a> or through the use of <a href="/wiki/Entheogen" title="Entheogen">entheogens</a>. The methods employed are diverse, and are often used together.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Entheogens">Entheogens</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Shamanism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Entheogens">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Flowering_San_Pedro_cactus.jpg"><img alt="text" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2048" data-file-width="1536" height="293" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a2/Flowering_San_Pedro_cactus.jpg/220px-Flowering_San_Pedro_cactus.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a2/Flowering_San_Pedro_cactus.jpg/330px-Flowering_San_Pedro_cactus.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a2/Flowering_San_Pedro_cactus.jpg/440px-Flowering_San_Pedro_cactus.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Flowering_San_Pedro_cactus.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Flowering <a href="/wiki/Echinopsis_pachanoi" title="Echinopsis pachanoi">San Pedro</a>, an entheogenic cactus that has been used for over 3,000 years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup> Today the vast majority of extracted mescaline is from columnar cacti, not vulnerable <a href="/wiki/Peyote" title="Peyote">peyote</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>An entheogen ("generating the divine within")<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup> is a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Psychoactive" title="Psychoactive">psychoactive</a> substance used in a <a href="/wiki/Religion" title="Religion">religious</a>, shamanic, or <a href="/wiki/Spirituality" title="Spirituality">spiritual</a> context.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup> Entheogens have been used in a <a href="/wiki/Ritual" title="Ritual">ritualized</a> context for thousands of years; their religious significance is well established in anthropological and modern evidences. Examples of traditional entheogens include: <a href="/wiki/Peyote" title="Peyote">peyote</a>, <a href="/wiki/Psilocybin_mushroom" title="Psilocybin mushroom">psilocybin mushrooms</a>, uncured <a href="/wiki/Tobacco" title="Tobacco">tobacco</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cannabis_(drug)" title="Cannabis (drug)">cannabis</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ayahuasca" title="Ayahuasca">ayahuasca</a>, <i><a href="/wiki/Salvia_divinorum" title="Salvia divinorum">Salvia divinorum</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Tabernanthe_iboga" title="Tabernanthe iboga">Tabernanthe iboga</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Ipomoea_tricolor" title="Ipomoea tricolor">Ipomoea tricolor</a></i>, and <i><a href="/wiki/Amanita_muscaria" title="Amanita muscaria">Amanita muscaria</a></i>.</p>
<p>Some shamans observe dietary or customary restrictions particular to their tradition. These restrictions are more than just cultural. For example, the diet followed by shamans and apprentices prior to participating in an <a href="/wiki/Ayahuasca" title="Ayahuasca">ayahuasca</a> ceremony includes foods rich in <a href="/wiki/Tryptophan" title="Tryptophan">tryptophan</a> (a biosynthetic precursor to <a href="/wiki/Serotonin" title="Serotonin">serotonin</a>) as well as avoiding foods rich in <a href="/wiki/Tyramine" title="Tyramine">tyramine</a>, which could induce <a href="/wiki/Hypertensive_crisis" title="Hypertensive crisis">hypertensive crisis</a> if ingested with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/MAOI" title="MAOI">MAOIs</a> such as are found in ayahuasca brews as well as abstinence from alcohol or sex.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Peru_57-2"><a href="#cite_note-Peru-57">[57]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Music_and_songs">Music and songs</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Shamanism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Music and songs">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Shamanic_music" title="Shamanic music">Shamanic music</a> and <a href="/wiki/Imitation_of_sounds_in_shamanism" title="Imitation of sounds in shamanism">Imitation of sounds in shamanism</a></div>
<p>Just like shamanism itself,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cogmap_15-1"><a href="#cite_note-cogmap-15">[15]</a></sup> music and songs related to it in various cultures are diverse, far from being alike. In several instances, songs related to shamanism are intended to imitate <a href="/wiki/Natural_sounds" title="Natural sounds">natural sounds</a>, via <a href="/wiki/Onomatopoeia" title="Onomatopoeia">onomatopoeia</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-onom_70-0"><a href="#cite_note-onom-70">[70]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Sound_mimesis_in_various_cultures" title="Sound mimesis in various cultures">Sound mimesis in various cultures</a> may serve other functions not necessarily related to shamanism: practical goals as luring game in the hunt;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-natt_71-0"><a href="#cite_note-natt-71">[71]</a></sup> or entertainment (<a href="/wiki/Inuit_throat_singing" title="Inuit throat singing">Inuit throat singing</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-natt_71-1"><a href="#cite_note-natt-71">[71]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-desch_72-0"><a href="#cite_note-desch-72">[72]</a></sup></p>
