<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Paraphernalia">Paraphernalia</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Shamanism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Paraphernalia">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Raven_Rattle,_19th_century,_05.588.7292.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1217" data-file-width="1536" height="174" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/65/Raven_Rattle%2C_19th_century%2C_05.588.7292.jpg/220px-Raven_Rattle%2C_19th_century%2C_05.588.7292.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/65/Raven_Rattle%2C_19th_century%2C_05.588.7292.jpg/330px-Raven_Rattle%2C_19th_century%2C_05.588.7292.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/65/Raven_Rattle%2C_19th_century%2C_05.588.7292.jpg/440px-Raven_Rattle%2C_19th_century%2C_05.588.7292.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Raven_Rattle,_19th_century,_05.588.7292.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Raven Rattle, 19th century, <a href="/wiki/Brooklyn_Museum" title="Brooklyn Museum">Brooklyn Museum</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Shamans may have various kinds of paraphernalia in different cultures.</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Goldes_shaman_priest_in_his_regalia.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="489" data-file-width="621" height="173" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5e/Goldes_shaman_priest_in_his_regalia.png/220px-Goldes_shaman_priest_in_his_regalia.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5e/Goldes_shaman_priest_in_his_regalia.png/330px-Goldes_shaman_priest_in_his_regalia.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5e/Goldes_shaman_priest_in_his_regalia.png/440px-Goldes_shaman_priest_in_his_regalia.png 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Goldes_shaman_priest_in_his_regalia.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Golds_(ethnic_group)" title="Golds (ethnic group)">Goldes</a> shaman priest in his regalia</div>
</div>
</div>
<ul>
<li><b>Drum</b> – The <a href="/wiki/Drum" title="Drum">drum</a> is used by shamans of several peoples in Siberia, the Inuit, and many other cultures all over the world,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup> although its usage for shamanistic <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Seance" title="Seance">seances</a> may be lacking among the Inuit of Canada.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup> The beating of the drum allows the shaman to achieve an altered state of consciousness or to travel on a journey between the physical and spiritual worlds.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Maxfield.2C_Melinda_1994_75-0"><a href="#cite_note-Maxfield.2C_Melinda_1994-75">[75]</a></sup> Much fascination surrounds the role that the acoustics of the drum play to the shaman. Shaman drums are generally constructed of an animal-skin stretched over a bent wooden hoop, with a handle across the hoop.</li>
<li><b>Feathers</b> – In numerous North and South American cultures, as well as in Europe and Asia, birds are seen as messengers of the spirits. Feathers are often used in ceremonies and in individual healing rituals.</li>
<li><b>Rattle</b> – Found mostly among South American<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rattle-south_76-0"><a href="#cite_note-rattle-south-76">[76]</a></sup> and African peoples. Also used in ceremonies among the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Navajo_people" title="Navajo people">Navajo</a> and in traditional ways in their blessings and ceremonies.</li>
<li><b>Gong</b> – Often found through South East Asia, Far Eastern peoples.</li>
<li><b>Pipe</b> – Used for smoking various tobaccos and psychoactive herbs (e.g. tobacco in North and South America, cannabis in Eurasia).</li>
<li><b>Sword</b> – In <a href="/wiki/Hmong_customs_and_culture#Shamanism" title="Hmong customs and culture">Hmong Shamanism</a>, a holy sword will always be used in the practice to protect the shaman from wandering "evil" spirits as he travels to the spirit world.</li>
<li><b>Shake</b> – Found mostly in Hmong Shamanism, the shaman begins his practice by rattling, which turns into a shake. It is the process of communicating with his shamanistic spirits to guide him to the spirit world.</li>
<li><b>Long Table</b> – A flexible wooden table, approximately nine by two feet, is used in Hmong Shamanism; the table transforms into a "flying horse" in the spirit world.</li>
<li><b>Rooster</b> – A rooster is often used in Hmong Shamanism. A shaman uses a rooster when he journeys to the unknown. It is said that the rooster shields the shaman from wandering "evil" spirits by making him invisible; thus, the evil spirits only see the rooster's useless spirit.</li>
</ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Academic_study">Academic study</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Shamanism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Academic study">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Shaman.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1792" data-file-width="1313" height="300" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d1/Shaman.jpg/220px-Shaman.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d1/Shaman.jpg/330px-Shaman.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d1/Shaman.jpg/440px-Shaman.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Shaman.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Sami_people" title="Sami people">Sami</a> shaman with his drum</div>
</div>
</div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Cognitive.2C_semiotic.2C_hermeneutic_approaches">Cognitive, semiotic, hermeneutic approaches</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Shamanism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Cognitive, semiotic, hermeneutic approaches">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>As mentioned, a (debated) approach explains the etymology of the word "shaman" as meaning "one who knows".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Di.C3.B3szegi_1962:13_16-1"><a href="#cite_note-Di.C3.B3szegi_1962:13-16">[16]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hopp.C3.A1l_2005:14_77-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hopp.C3.A1l_2005:14-77">[77]</a></sup> Functionally, the shaman is a person who is an expert in keeping together the multiple <a href="/wiki/Code" title="Code">codes</a> of the society. Accordingly, the society's codes are the manifestation of the society's underlying complex belief system. Thus to be effective, shamans maintain a comprehensive view in their mind which gives them certainty of <a href="/wiki/Knowledge" title="Knowledge">knowledge</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cogmap_15-2"><a href="#cite_note-cogmap-15">[15]</a></sup> The shaman uses (and the audience understands) multiple <a href="/wiki/Code" title="Code">codes</a>. Shamans express meanings in many ways: verbally, musically, artistically, and in dance. Meanings may be manifested in objects such as <a href="/wiki/Amulet" title="Amulet">amulets</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hopp.C3.A1l_2005:14_77-1"><a href="#cite_note-Hopp.C3.A1l_2005:14-77">[77]</a></sup></p>
<p>The shaman knows the <a href="/wiki/Culture" title="Culture">culture</a> of his or her community well,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mediator2_29-1"><a href="#cite_note-mediator2-29">[29]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-knowncult_79-0"><a href="#cite_note-knowncult-79">[79]</a></sup> and acts accordingly. Thus, their audience knows the used <a href="/wiki/Symbol" title="Symbol">symbols</a> and meanings—that is why shamanism can be efficient: people in the audience trust it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-knowncult_79-1"><a href="#cite_note-knowncult-79">[79]</a></sup> For example, the shaman's <a href="/wiki/Drum" title="Drum">drumming</a> can appear to its members as certainty of <i><a href="/wiki/Knowledge" title="Knowledge">knowledge</a></i>—this explains the above described etymology for the word "shaman" as meaning "one who knows."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-etym2_80-0"><a href="#cite_note-etym2-80">[80]</a></sup></p>
<p>There are <a href="/wiki/Semiotics" title="Semiotics">semiotic</a> theoretical approaches to shamanism,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-semi_81-0"><a href="#cite_note-semi-81">[81]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup> ("<a href="/wiki/Ethnosemiotics" title="Ethnosemiotics">ethnosemiotics</a>"). The symbols on the shaman's costume and drum can refer to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Power_animal" title="Power animal">Power animals</a>, or to the rank of the shaman.</p>
<p>There are also examples of "mutually opposing symbols", distinguishing a "white" shaman who contacts sky spirits for good aims by day, from a "black" shaman who contacts evil spirits for bad aims by night.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-semiotics_of_shamanism_84-0"><a href="#cite_note-semiotics_of_shamanism-84">[84]</a></sup> (Series of such opposing symbols referred to a world-view behind them. Analogously to the way grammar arranges words to express meanings and convey a world, also this formed a cognitive map).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cogmap_15-3"><a href="#cite_note-cogmap-15">[15]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hop-natworsib_85-0"><a href="#cite_note-hop-natworsib-85">[85]</a></sup> Shaman's lore is rooted in the folklore of the community, which provides a "mythological mental map".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hopp.C3.A1l_2007c_25_87-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hopp.C3.A1l_2007c_25-87">[87]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Juha_Pentik%C3%A4inen" title="Juha Pentikäinen">Juha Pentikäinen</a> uses the concept <i>"grammar of mind"</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hopp.C3.A1l_2007c_25_87-1"><a href="#cite_note-Hopp.C3.A1l_2007c_25-87">[87]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup> Linking to a Sami example, Kathleen Osgood Dana writes:</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>Juha Pentikäinen, in his introduction to Shamanism and Northern Ecology, explains how the Sámi drum embodies Sámi worldviews. He considers shamanism to be a <i>‘grammar of mind’</i> (10), because shamans need to be experts in the folklore of their cultures (11).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dan-aill_89-0"><a href="#cite_note-dan-aill-89">[89]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<p>Armin Geertz coined and introduced the <a href="/wiki/Hermeneutics" title="Hermeneutics">hermeneutics</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup> "ethnohermeneutics",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hop-natworsib_85-1"><a href="#cite_note-hop-natworsib-85">[85]</a></sup> approaches to the practice of interpretation. Hoppál extended the term to include not only the interpretation of oral and written texts, but that of "visual texts as well (including motions, gestures and more complex ritual, and ceremonies performed for instance by shamans)".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ethnohermeneutics_91-0"><a href="#cite_note-ethnohermeneutics-91">[91]</a></sup> It not only reveals the <a href="/wiki/Animism" title="Animism">animistic</a> views hiding behind shamanism, but also conveys their relevance for the contemporary world, where ecological problems have validated paradigms about balance and protection.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hopp.C3.A1l_2007c_25_87-2"><a href="#cite_note-Hopp.C3.A1l_2007c_25-87">[87]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ecological_approaches.2C_systems_theory">Ecological approaches, systems theory</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Shamanism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Ecological approaches, systems theory">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Other <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fieldwork" title="Fieldwork">fieldworks</a> use <a href="/wiki/Systems_theory" title="Systems theory">systems theory</a> concepts and ecological considerations to understand the shaman's <a href="/wiki/Folklore" title="Folklore">lore</a>. Desana and <a href="/wiki/Tucano_people" title="Tucano people">Tucano</a> Indians have developed a sophisticated symbolism and concepts of "energy" flowing between people and animals in cyclic paths. <a href="/wiki/Gerardo_Reichel-Dolmatoff" title="Gerardo Reichel-Dolmatoff">Gerardo Reichel-Dolmatoff</a> relates these concepts to developments in the ways that modern science (systems theory, ecology, new approaches in anthropology and archeology) treats <a href="/wiki/Causality" title="Causality">causality</a> in a less linear fashion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-eco_50-1"><a href="#cite_note-eco-50">[50]</a></sup> He also suggests a cooperation of modern science and indigenous lore.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-coop_92-0"><a href="#cite_note-coop-92">[92]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Hypotheses_on_origins">Hypotheses on origins</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Shamanism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Hypotheses on origins">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Shamanic practices may originate as early as the <a href="/wiki/Paleolithic" title="Paleolithic">Paleolithic</a>, predating all organized religions,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Narr_94-0"><a href="#cite_note-Narr-94">[94]</a></sup> and certainly as early as the <a href="/wiki/Neolithic" title="Neolithic">Neolithic</a> period.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Narr_94-1"><a href="#cite_note-Narr-94">[94]</a></sup> The earliest known undisputed burial of a shaman (and by extension the earliest undisputed evidence of shamans and shamanic practices) dates back to the early <a href="/wiki/Upper_Paleolithic" title="Upper Paleolithic">Upper Paleolithic era</a> (c. 30,000 BP) in what is now the Czech Republic.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[95]</a></sup></p>
<p>Sanskrit scholar and comparative mythologist <a href="/wiki/Michael_Witzel" title="Michael Witzel">Michael Witzel</a> proposes that all of the world's mythologies, and also the concepts and practices of shamans, can be traced to the migrations of two prehistoric populations: the "Gondwana" type (of circa 65,000 years ago) and the "Laurasian" type (of circa 40,000 years ago).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[96]</a></sup> The more recent Laurasian types of myths and forms of shamanism are found in Eurasian and North and South America and are later cultural elaborations based upon the earlier Gondwana types of myths and shamanism, both of which probably derived from an earlier human source population. Witzel argues that survivals of the older, original forms of shamanism are therefore to be found in the southern hemisphere among peoples such as the San Bushmen of Botswana, the Andamanese of the Andaman Islands off the coast of Burma, and the Aborigines of Australia. The so-called "classical" shamanism of Siberia and the Americas reflect a further cultural evolutionary development at the local levels.</p>
<p>Early anthropological studies theorize that shamanism developed as a magic practice to ensure a successful hunt or gathering of food. Evidence in caves and drawings on walls support indications that shamanism started during the Paleolithic era. One such picture featured a half-animal, with the face and legs of a man, with antlers and a tail of a stag.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[97]</a></sup></p>
<p>Archaeological evidence exists for <a href="/wiki/Mesolithic" title="Mesolithic">Mesolithic</a> shamanism. The oldest known shaman grave in the world is located in the Czech Republic at Dolni Vestonice (National Geographic No 174 October 1988). This grave site was evidence of a female shaman.</p>
<p>In November 2008, researchers from the Hebrew University of Jerusalem announced the discovery of a 12,000-year-old site in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pre-history_of_the_Southern_Levant" title="Pre-history of the Southern Levant">Israel</a> that is perceived as one of the earliest known shaman burials. The elderly woman had been arranged on her side, with her legs apart and folded inward at the knee. Ten large stones were placed on the head, pelvis and arms. Among her unusual <a href="/wiki/Grave_goods" title="Grave goods">grave goods</a> were 50 complete tortoise shells, a human foot, and certain body parts from animals such as a cow tail and eagle wings. Other animal remains came from a boar, leopard, and two martens. "It seems that the woman … was perceived as being in a close relationship with these animal spirits", researchers noted. The grave was one of at least 28 graves at the site, located in a cave in lower <a href="/wiki/Galilee" title="Galilee">Galilee</a> and belonging to the <a href="/wiki/Natufian_culture" title="Natufian culture">Natufian culture</a>, but is said to be unlike any other among the Epipaleolithic Natufians or in the Paleolithic period.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[98]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Robert_Sapolsky" title="Robert Sapolsky">Robert Sapolsky</a> has theorized that shamanism is practiced by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Schizotypal" title="Schizotypal">schizotypal</a> individuals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[99]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Historical-anthropological_school_of_folkloristics">Historical-anthropological school of folkloristics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Shamanism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Historical-anthropological school of folkloristics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Folklorists have evaluated the presence of remnants of shamanism and shamanic practice in <a href="/wiki/Folklore" title="Folklore">folktales</a> from around the world. Michael Berman identified the genre of the <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Shamanic_Story&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Shamanic Story (page does not exist)">shamanic story</a>, examples of which are only produced by folk groups with shamanic cosmology or a shamanic world view. Kultkrantz points out that, "in areas where shamanism has long been a thing of the past, many tales contain only vague, piecemeal or inaccurate recollections of shamans and their like."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[100]</a></sup> The presence of distinctive characteristics and features of shamanic stories help folklorists and anthropologists reconstruct a culture’s practice of shamanism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[101]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Decline_and_revitalization_.2F_tradition-preserving_movements">Decline and revitalization / tradition-preserving movements</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Shamanism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Decline and revitalization / tradition-preserving movements">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Shamanism is believed to be declining around the world, possibly due to other organised religious influences, like Christianity, that want people who practice shamanism to convert to their own system and doctrine. Another reason is western views of shamanism as 'primitive', 'superstitious', backward and outdated. Whalers who frequently interact with Inuit tribes are one source of this decline in that region.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[102]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Kyzyl_Shaman.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="800" data-file-width="420" height="419" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4f/Kyzyl_Shaman.jpg/220px-Kyzyl_Shaman.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4f/Kyzyl_Shaman.jpg/330px-Kyzyl_Shaman.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/4/4f/Kyzyl_Shaman.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Kyzyl_Shaman.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A recent photograph: shaman doctor of <a href="/wiki/Kyzyl" title="Kyzyl">Kyzyl</a>, 2005. (Details missing). Attempts are being made to preserve and revitalize <a href="/wiki/Tuvans" title="Tuvans">Tuvan</a> shamanism:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-tuva_103-0"><a href="#cite_note-tuva-103">[103]</a></sup> former authentic shamans have begun to practice again, and young apprentices are being educated in an organized way.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-authentic-revitalization-tuva_104-0"><a href="#cite_note-authentic-revitalization-tuva-104">[104]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In many areas, former shamans ceased to fulfill the functions in the community they used to, as they felt mocked by their own community,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105">[105]</a></sup> or regarded their own past as deprecated and are unwilling to talk about it to an ethnographer.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-shamed_106-0"><a href="#cite_note-shamed-106">[106]</a></sup></p>
<p>Moreover, besides personal communications of former shamans, folklore texts may narrate directly about a deterioration process. For example, a <a href="/wiki/Buryats" title="Buryats">Buryat</a> epic text details the wonderful deeds of the ancient "first shaman" Kara-Gürgän:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kara-Gurgan_107-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kara-Gurgan-107">[107]</a></sup> he could even compete with God, create life, steal back the soul of the sick from God without his consent. A subsequent text laments that shamans of older times were stronger, possessing capabilities like <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Omnividence&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Omnividence (page does not exist)">omnividence</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-flatland_108-0"><a href="#cite_note-flatland-108">[108]</a></sup> fortune-telling even for decades in the future, moving as fast as a bullet.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-deter_109-0"><a href="#cite_note-deter-109">[109]</a></sup></p>
<p>In most affected areas, shamanic practices ceased to exist, with authentic shamans dying and their personal experiences dying with them. The loss of memories is not always lessened by the fact the shaman is not always the only person in a community who knows the beliefs and motives related to the local shaman-hood (laics know myths as well, among Barasana, even though less;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-barasana-myths_44-1"><a href="#cite_note-barasana-myths-44">[44]</a></sup> there are former shaman apprentices unable to complete the learning among Greenlandic Inuit peoples,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-failed-shaman_47-2"><a href="#cite_note-failed-shaman-47">[47]</a></sup> moreover, even laics can have trance-like experiences among the Inuit;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mer-BecHalfHid_45-2"><a href="#cite_note-Mer-BecHalfHid-45">[45]</a></sup> the assistant of a shaman can be extremely knowledgeable among <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dagara_people" title="Dagara people">Dagara</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Guan_Kouni_48-1"><a href="#cite_note-Guan_Kouni-48">[48]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-interpreter_49-1"><a href="#cite_note-interpreter-49">[49]</a></sup> Although the shaman is often believed and trusted precisely because s/he "accommodates" to the "grammar" of the beliefs of the community,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-knowncult_79-2"><a href="#cite_note-knowncult-79">[79]</a></sup> several parts of the knowledge related to the local shamanhood consist of personal experiences of the shaman (illness), or root in his/her family life (the interpretation of the symbolics of his/her drum),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-drum-symbols_110-0"><a href="#cite_note-drum-symbols-110">[110]</a></sup> thus, those are lost with his/her death. Besides that, in many cultures, the entire traditional belief system has become endangered (often together with a partial or total <a href="/wiki/Language_shift" title="Language shift">language shift</a>), the other people of the community remembering the associated beliefs and practices (or the language at all) grew old or died, many folklore memories (songs, texts) were forgotten – which may threaten even such peoples who could preserve their isolation until the middle of the 20th century, like the <a href="/wiki/Nganasan_people" title="Nganasan people">Nganasan</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111"><a href="#cite_note-111">[111]</a></sup></p>
<p>Some areas could enjoy a prolonged resistance due to their remoteness.</p>
<ul>
<li>Variants of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shamanism_among_Inuit_peoples" title="Shamanism among Inuit peoples">shamanism among Inuit peoples</a> were once a widespread (and very diverse) phenomenon, but today are rarely practiced, as well as already having been in decline among many groups, even while the first major ethnological research was being done,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112"><a href="#cite_note-112">[112]</a></sup> e.g. among Polar Inuit, at the end of 19th century, <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Sagloq&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Sagloq (page does not exist)">Sagloq</a>, the last shaman who was believed to be able to travel to the sky and under the sea died—and many other former shamanic capacities were lost during that time as well, like <a href="/wiki/Ventriloquism" title="Ventriloquism">ventriloquism</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sleight-of-hand" title="Sleight-of-hand">sleight-of-hand</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113"><a href="#cite_note-113">[113]</a></sup></li>
<li>The isolated location of <a href="/wiki/Nganasan_people" title="Nganasan people">Nganasan people</a> allowed shamanism to be a living phenomenon among them even at the beginning of 20th century,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hop_114-0"><a href="#cite_note-hop-114">[114]</a></sup> the last notable Nganasan shaman's ceremonies could be recorded on film in the 1970s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hoppal_115-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hoppal-115">[115]</a></sup></li>
</ul>
<p>After exemplifying the general decline even in the most remote areas, it should be noted that there are revitalization or tradition-preserving efforts as a response. Besides collecting the memories,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-collect_116-0"><a href="#cite_note-collect-116">[116]</a></sup> there are also tradition-preserving<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-117"><a href="#cite_note-117">[117]</a></sup> and even revitalization efforts,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-118"><a href="#cite_note-118">[118]</a></sup> led by authentic former shamans (for example among <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sakha_people" title="Sakha people">Sakha people</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-authentic-revitalization-sakha_119-0"><a href="#cite_note-authentic-revitalization-sakha-119">[119]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Tuvans" title="Tuvans">Tuvans</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-authentic-revitalization-tuva_104-1"><a href="#cite_note-authentic-revitalization-tuva-104">[104]</a></sup> However, according to Richard L. Allen, Research &amp; Policy Analyst for the Cherokee Nation, they are overwhelmed with <a href="/wiki/Plastic_shaman" title="Plastic shaman">fraudulent shamans</a>, also known as plastic medicine people.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-120"><a href="#cite_note-120">[120]</a></sup> "One may assume that anyone claiming to be a Cherokee 'shaman, spiritual healer, or pipe-carrier', is equivalent to a modern day medicine show and snake-oil vendor."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RG_121-0"><a href="#cite_note-RG-121">[121]</a></sup> One indicator of a plastic shaman might be someone who discusses "Native American spirituality" but does not mention any specific <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Federally_recognized_tribes" title="Federally recognized tribes">Native American tribe</a>. The "New Age Frauds and Plastic Shamans" website discusses potentially plastic shamans.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122"><a href="#cite_note-122">[122]</a></sup></p>
<p>Besides tradition-preserving efforts, there are also <a href="/wiki/Neoshamanism" title="Neoshamanism">neoshamanistic</a> movements, these may differ from many traditional shamanistic practice and beliefs in several points.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-neoshaman_123-0"><a href="#cite_note-neoshaman-123">[123]</a></sup> Admittedly,<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Words_to_watch#Unsupported_attributions" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Words to watch"><span title="The material near this tag may use weasel words or too-vague attribution. (February 2012)">according to whom?</span></a></i>]</sup> several traditional beliefs systems indeed have ecological considerations (for example, many Inuit peoples), and among <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tukano_people" title="Tukano people">Tukano people</a>, the shaman indeed has direct resource-protecting roles, see details in section <a href="#Ecological_aspect">Ecological aspect</a>.</p>
<p>Today, shamanism survives primarily among <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Indigenous_people" title="Indigenous people">indigenous peoples</a>. Shamanic practices continue today in the tundras, jungles, deserts, and other rural areas, and even in cities, towns, suburbs, and shantytowns all over the world. This is especially true for Africa and South America, where "<a href="/wiki/Mestizo" title="Mestizo">mestizo</a> shamanism" is widespread.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Regional_variations">Regional variations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Shamanism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Regional variations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Asia">Asia</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Shamanism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Asia">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Hmong_shamanism">Hmong shamanism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Shamanism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Hmong shamanism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Hmong_customs_and_culture#Shamanism" title="Hmong customs and culture">Hmong customs and culture § Shamanism</a></div>
<p>The Hmong people,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-124"><a href="#cite_note-124">[124]</a></sup> as an ancient people of China with a 5,000-year history, continue to maintain and practice its form of shamanism known as "Ua Neeb" in mainland Asia. At the end of the Vietnam War, some 300,000 Hmong have been settled across the globe. They have continued to practice Ua Neeb in various countries in North and South America, Europe and Australia. In the U.S., the Hmong shaman practitioner is known as "Txiv Neeb" has been licensed by many hospitals in California as being part of the medical health team to treat patients in hospital. This revival of Ua Neeb in the West has been brought great success and has been hailed in the media as "Doctor for the disease, shaman for the soul".</p>
<p>Being a Hmong shaman represents a true vocation, chosen by the shaman God "Sivyis".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-125"><a href="#cite_note-125">[125]</a></sup> The Shaman's main job is to bring harmony to the individual, their family, and their community within their environment by performing various rituals (usually through trance).</p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Animal_sacrifice" title="Animal sacrifice">Animal sacrifice</a> has been part of the Hmong shamanic practice for the past 5,000 years. Contrary to the belief of many Westerners, the Hmong practice of using animals in shamanic practice is performed with great respect. After the Vietnam War, over 200,000 Hmong were resettled in the United States and shamanism is still part of the Hmong culture. Due the colliding of culture and the law, as Professor Alison Dundes Renteln, a political science professor at the University of Southern California and author of The Cultural Defense, a book that examines the influence of such cases on U.S. courts, once said, "We say that as a society we welcome diversity, and in fact that we embrace it ... In practice, it's not that easy".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-usatoday.com_126-0"><a href="#cite_note-usatoday.com-126">[126]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Hmong believe that all things on Earth have a soul (or multiple souls) and those souls are treated as equal and can be considered interchangeable. When a person is sick due to his soul being lost, or captured by wild spirit, it is necessary to ask for and receive permission of that animal, whether it is a chicken, pig, dog, goat or any other animals required, to use its soul for an exchange with the afflicted person's soul for a period of 12 months. At the end of that period, during the Hmong New Year, the shaman would perform a special ritual to release the soul of that animal and send it off to the world beyond. As part of his service to mankind, the animal soul is sent off to be reincarnated into a higher form of animal, or even to become a member of a god's family (ua Fuab Tais Ntuj tus tub, tus ntxhais) to live a life of luxury, free of the suffering as an animal. Hence, being asked to perform this duty (what is known in the West as "animal sacrifice") is one of the greatest honors for that animal, to be able to serve mankind. The Hmong of Southeast Guizhou will cover the <a href="/wiki/Rooster" title="Rooster">cock</a> with a piece of red cloth and then hold it up to worship and sacrifice to the Heaven and the Earth before the <a href="/wiki/Rooster#Religion_and_spiritual_belief_systems" title="Rooster">Sacred cockfight</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-127"><a href="#cite_note-127">[127]</a></sup> In a 2010 trial of a Sheboygan Wisconsin Hmong who was charged with staging a cockfight, it was stated that the roosters were "kept for both food and religious purposes",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Midwest_Communications_Inc_128-0"><a href="#cite_note-Midwest_Communications_Inc-128">[128]</a></sup> and the case was followed by an acquittal.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Midwest_Communications_Inc_128-1"><a href="#cite_note-Midwest_Communications_Inc-128">[128]</a></sup></p>
<p>In addition to the spiritual dimension, Hmong shaman attempt to treat many physical illnesses through use of the text of sacred words (khawv koob).</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Indonesia">Indonesia</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Shamanism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Indonesia">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Dukun" title="Dukun">Dukun</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:COLLECTIE_TROPENMUSEUM_Een_dukun_tijdens_de_bereiding_van_zijn_geneesmiddelen_TMnr_60027035.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="509" data-file-width="700" height="160" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/85/COLLECTIE_TROPENMUSEUM_Een_dukun_tijdens_de_bereiding_van_zijn_geneesmiddelen_TMnr_60027035.jpg/220px-COLLECTIE_TROPENMUSEUM_Een_dukun_tijdens_de_bereiding_van_zijn_geneesmiddelen_TMnr_60027035.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/85/COLLECTIE_TROPENMUSEUM_Een_dukun_tijdens_de_bereiding_van_zijn_geneesmiddelen_TMnr_60027035.jpg/330px-COLLECTIE_TROPENMUSEUM_Een_dukun_tijdens_de_bereiding_van_zijn_geneesmiddelen_TMnr_60027035.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/85/COLLECTIE_TROPENMUSEUM_Een_dukun_tijdens_de_bereiding_van_zijn_geneesmiddelen_TMnr_60027035.jpg/440px-COLLECTIE_TROPENMUSEUM_Een_dukun_tijdens_de_bereiding_van_zijn_geneesmiddelen_TMnr_60027035.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:COLLECTIE_TROPENMUSEUM_Een_dukun_tijdens_de_bereiding_van_zijn_geneesmiddelen_TMnr_60027035.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Indonesian <a href="/wiki/Dukun" title="Dukun">Dukun</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Throughout the villages and towns of <a href="/wiki/Indonesia" title="Indonesia">Indonesia</a>, local healers known as <a href="/wiki/Dukun" title="Dukun">dukun</a> practice diverse activities from massage, bonesetting, midwivery, herbal medicine, spirit mediumship and divination.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Japan">Japan</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Shamanism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Japan">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Miko" title="Miko">Miko</a></div>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Shinto" title="Shinto">Shinto</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ainu_religion" title="Ainu religion">Ainu religion</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Ryukyuan_religion" title="Ryukyuan religion">Ryukyuan religion</a></div>
<p>Shamanism is part of the indigenous Ainu religion and Japanese religion of <a href="/wiki/Shinto" title="Shinto">Shinto</a>, although Shinto is distinct in that it is shamanism for an agricultural society. Since the early middle-ages Shinto has been influenced by and <a href="/wiki/Syncretism" title="Syncretism">syncretized</a> with Buddhism and other elements of continental East Asian culture. The book "<i>Occult Japan: Shinto, Shamanism and the Way of the Gods</i>" by Percival Lowell delves further into researching Japanese shamanism or Shintoism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-129"><a href="#cite_note-129">[129]</a></sup> The book <i>Japan Through the Looking Glass: Shaman to Shinto</i> uncovers the extraordinary aspects of Japanese beliefs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-130"><a href="#cite_note-130">[130]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-131"><a href="#cite_note-131">[131]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Korea">Korea</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Shamanism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Korea">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Korean_shamanism" title="Korean shamanism">Korean shamanism</a></div>
<p>Shamanism is still practiced in <a href="/wiki/North_Korea" title="North Korea">North</a> and <a href="/wiki/South_Korea" title="South Korea">South Korea</a>. In the south, shaman women are known as <i>mudangs</i>, while male shamans are referred to as <i>baksoo mudangs</i>.</p>
<p>A person can become a shaman through hereditary title or through natural ability. Shamans are consulted in contemporary society for financial and marital decisions.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Malaysia">Malaysia</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Shamanism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Malaysia">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Bobohizan" title="Bobohizan">Bobohizan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bomoh" title="Bomoh">Bomoh</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Pawang" title="Pawang">Pawang</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Bobohizans.jpeg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1475" data-file-width="2362" height="137" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/Bobohizans.jpeg/220px-Bobohizans.jpeg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/Bobohizans.jpeg/330px-Bobohizans.jpeg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/Bobohizans.jpeg/440px-Bobohizans.jpeg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Bobohizans.jpeg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<i><a href="/wiki/Bobohizan" title="Bobohizan">Bobohizan</a></i> of <a href="/wiki/North_Borneo" title="North Borneo">North Borneo</a>, circa 1921.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Shamanism were also practiced among the Malay community in <a href="/wiki/Malay_Peninsula" title="Malay Peninsula">Malay Peninsula</a> and indigenous people in <a href="/wiki/Sabah" title="Sabah">Sabah</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sarawak" title="Sarawak">Sarawak</a>. People who practice shamanism in the country are generally called as <i>bomoh</i> or <i>pawang</i> in the Peninsula.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-132"><a href="#cite_note-132">[132]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-133"><a href="#cite_note-133">[133]</a></sup> In Sabah, the <a href="/wiki/Bobohizan" title="Bobohizan">Bobohizan</a> is the main shaman among the <a href="/wiki/Kadazan-Dusun" title="Kadazan-Dusun">Kadazan-Dusun</a> indigenous community.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-134"><a href="#cite_note-134">[134]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Mongolia">Mongolia</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Shamanism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Mongolia">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Mongolian_shamanism" title="Mongolian shamanism">Mongolian shamanism</a></div>
<p>Mongolian classics, such as <i><a href="/wiki/The_Secret_History_of_the_Mongols" title="The Secret History of the Mongols">The Secret History of the Mongols</a></i>, provide details about male and female shamans serving as exorcists, healers, rainmakers, oneiromancers, soothsayers, and officials. Shamanic practices continue in present-day Mongolian culture.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-135"><a href="#cite_note-135">[135]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-136"><a href="#cite_note-136">[136]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-137"><a href="#cite_note-137">[137]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-138"><a href="#cite_note-138">[138]</a></sup></p>
<p>The spiritual hierarchy in clan-based Mongolian society was complex. The highest group consisted of 99 <i>tngri</i> (55 of them benevolent or "white" and 44 terrifying or "black"), 77 <i>natigai</i> or "earth-mothers", besides others. The <i>tngri</i> were called upon only by leaders and great shamans and were common to all the clans. After these, three groups of ancestral spirits dominated. The "Lord-Spirits" were the souls of clan leaders to whom any member of a clan could appeal for physical or spiritual help. The "Protector-Spirits" included the souls of great shamans (<i>ĵigari</i>) and shamanesses (<i>abĵiya</i>). The "Guardian-Spirits" were made up of the souls of smaller shamans (<i>böge</i>) and shamanesses (<i>idugan</i>) and were associated with a specific locality (including mountains, rivers, etc.) in the clan's territory.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-139"><a href="#cite_note-139">[139]</a></sup></p>
<p>In the 1990s, a form of Mongolian neo-shamanism was created which has given a more modern approach to shamanism. Mongolian shamans are now making a business out of their profession and even have offices in the larger towns. At these businesses, a shaman generally heads the organization and performs services such as healing, fortunetelling, and solving all kinds of problems.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-140"><a href="#cite_note-140">[140]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Philippines">Philippines</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Shamanism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Philippines">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The Shaman in the Philippines is considered as a priest- sacrifice, healer, and intermediary with the spirit world. Shamans are also considered as prophets and seers. The magician or sorcerer can either be a white magician or a medicine man whose actions are for the good of others, while the black magician or witchdoctors can either do well or harm to people, but mostly harm for a fee. Shamans in the Philippines deal with various kinds of spirits and learn how to summon and control them. Once someone is declared a Shaman, he is declared to be a sick man, but not just any type of sick man. He or she is special because the shaman is able to learn how to cure himself. An epilepsy attack is the initiation of the Shaman; it is equivalent to them being cured. <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-141"><a href="#cite_note-141">[141]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Siberia_and_North_Asia">Siberia and North Asia</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Shamanism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Siberia and North Asia">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Shamanism_in_Siberia" title="Shamanism in Siberia">Shamanism in Siberia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Shamanism_in_the_Qing_dynasty" title="Shamanism in the Qing dynasty">the Qing Dynasty</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">

<p><a href="/wiki/Siberia" title="Siberia">Siberia</a> is regarded as the <i>locus classicus</i> of shamanism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-locclass_142-0"><a href="#cite_note-locclass-142">[142]</a></sup> The area is inhabited by many different ethnic groups, and many of its peoples observe shamanistic practices, even in modern times. Many classical ethnographic sources of "shamanism" were recorded among Siberian peoples.</p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Manchu_people" title="Manchu people">Manchu</a> Shamanism is one of very few Shamanist traditions which held official status into the modern era, by becoming one of the imperial cults of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Qing_Dynasty" title="Qing Dynasty">Qing Dynasty</a> of <a href="/wiki/China" title="China">China</a> (alongside <a href="/wiki/Buddhism" title="Buddhism">Buddhism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Taoism" title="Taoism">Taoism</a> and traditional <a href="/wiki/Heaven_worship" title="Heaven worship">Heaven worship</a>). The <a href="/wiki/Palace_of_Earthly_Tranquility" title="Palace of Earthly Tranquility">Palace of Earthly Tranquility</a>, one of the principal halls of the <a href="/wiki/Forbidden_City" title="Forbidden City">Forbidden City</a> in <a href="/wiki/Beijing" title="Beijing">Beijing</a>, was partly dedicated to Shamanistic rituals. The ritual set-up is still preserved <i>in situ</i> today.</p>
<p>Among the Siberian <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chukchis" title="Chukchis">Chukchis</a> peoples, a shaman is interpreted as someone who is <a href="/wiki/Spirit_possession" title="Spirit possession">possessed</a> by a spirit, who demands that someone assume the shamanic role for their people. Among the Buryat, there is a ritual known as "shanar"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-143"><a href="#cite_note-143">[143]</a></sup> whereby a candidate is consecrated as shaman by another, already-established shaman.</p>
<p>Among several <a href="/wiki/Samoyedic_peoples" title="Samoyedic peoples">Samoyedic peoples</a> shamanism was a living tradition also in modern times, especially at groups living in isolation, until recent times (<a href="/wiki/Nganasan_people" title="Nganasan people">Nganasans</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ngan_144-0"><a href="#cite_note-ngan-144">[144]</a></sup> The last notable Nganasan shaman's seances could be recorded on film in the 1970s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hoppal_115-1"><a href="#cite_note-Hoppal-115">[115]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ngan_144-1"><a href="#cite_note-ngan-144">[144]</a></sup></p>
<p>When the People's Republic of China was formed in 1949 and the border with Russian Siberia was formally sealed, many nomadic Tungus groups (including the Evenki) that practiced shamanism were confined in Manchuria and Inner Mongolia. The last shaman of the Oroqen, Chuonnasuan (Meng Jin Fu), died in October 2000.</p>
<p>In many other cases, shamanism was in decline even at the beginning of 20th century (<a href="/wiki/Romani_people" title="Romani people">Roma</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hoppal_a_30-1"><a href="#cite_note-Hoppal_a-30">[30]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Central_Asia">Central Asia</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Shamanism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Central Asia">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Geographic_influences_on_Central_Asian_shamanism">Geographic influences on Central Asian shamanism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Shamanism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Geographic influences on Central Asian shamanism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h5>
<p>Geographical factors heavily influence the character and development of the religion, myths, rituals and epics of Central Asia. While in other parts of the world, religious rituals are primarily used to promote agricultural prosperity, here they were used to ensure success in hunting and breeding livestock. Animals are one of the most important elements of indigenous religion in Central Asia because of the role they play in the survival of the nomadic civilizations of the steppes as well as sedentary populations living on land not conducive to agriculture. Shamans wore animal skins and feathers and underwent transformations into animals during spiritual journeys. In addition, animals served as humans' guides, rescuers, ancestors, totems and sacrificial victims.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-145"><a href="#cite_note-145">[145]</a></sup> As a religion of nature, shamanism throughout Central Asia held particular reverence for the relations between sky, earth and water and believed in the mystical importance of trees and mountains. Shamanism in Central Asia also places a strong emphasis on the opposition between summer and winter, corresponding to the huge differences in temperature common in the region. The harsh conditions and poverty caused by the extreme temperatures drove Central Asian nomads throughout history to pursue militaristic goals against their sedentary neighbors. This military background can be seen in the reverence for horses and warriors within many indigenous religions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-146"><a href="#cite_note-146">[146]</a></sup></p>
<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Common_shamanic_practices_and_beliefs_shared_among_Central_Asians">Common shamanic practices and beliefs shared among Central Asians</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Shamanism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: Common shamanic practices and beliefs shared among Central Asians">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h5>
<p>Central Asian shamans served as sacred intermediaries between the human and spirit world. In this role they took on tasks such as healing, divination, appealing to ancestors, manipulating the elements, leading lost souls and officiating public religious rituals. The shamanic séance served as a public display of the shaman's journey to the spirit world and usually involved intense trances, drumming, dancing, chanting, elaborate costumes, miraculous displays of physical strength, and audience involvement. The goal of these séances ranged from recovering the lost soul of a sick patient and divining the future to controlling the weather and finding a lost person or thing. The use of sleight-of-hand tricks, ventriloquism, and hypnosis were common in these rituals but did not explain the more impressive feats and actual cures accomplished by shamans.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-147"><a href="#cite_note-147">[147]</a></sup></p>
<p>Shamans perform in a "state of ecstasy" deliberately induced by an effort of will. Reaching this altered state of consciousness required great mental exertion, concentration and strict self-discipline. Mental and physical preparation included long periods of silent meditation, fasting, and smoking. In this state, skilled shamans employ capabilities that the human organism cannot accomplish in the ordinary state. Shamans in ecstasy displayed unusual physical strength, the ability to withstand extreme temperatures, the bearing of stabbing and cutting without pain, and the heightened receptivity of the sense organs. Shamans made use of intoxicating substances and hallucinogens, especially mukhomor mushrooms and alcohol, as a means of hastening the attainment of ecstasy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-148"><a href="#cite_note-148">[148]</a></sup></p>
<p>The use of purification by fire is an important element of the shamanic tradition dating back as early as the 6th century. People and things connected with the dead had to be purified by passing between fires. These purifications were complex exorcisms while others simply involved the act of literally walking between two fires while being blessed by the Shaman. Shamans in literature and practice were also responsible for using special stones to manipulate weather. Rituals are performed with these stones to attract rain or repel snow, cold or wind. This "rain-stone" was used for many occasions including bringing an end to drought as well as producing hailstorms as a means of warfare.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-149"><a href="#cite_note-149">[149]</a></sup> Despite distinctions between various types of shamans and specific traditions, there is a uniformity throughout the region manifested in the personal beliefs, objectives, rituals, symbols and the appearance of shamans.</p>
<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Shamanic_rituals_as_artistic_performance">Shamanic rituals as artistic performance</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Shamanism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: Shamanic rituals as artistic performance">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h5>
<p>The shamanic ceremony is both a religious ceremony and an artistic performance. The fundamental purpose of the dramatic displays seen during shamanic ceremonies is not to draw attention or to create a spectacle for the audience as many Westerners have come to believe, but to lead the tribe in a solemn ritualistic process.</p>
<p>In general, all performances consist of four elements: dance, music, poetry and dramatic or mimetic action. The use of these elements serves the purpose of outwardly expressing his mystical communion with nature and the spirits for the rest of the tribe. The true shaman can make the journey to the spirit world at any time and any place, but shamanic ceremonies provide a way for the rest of the tribe to share in this religious experience. The shaman changes his voice mimetically to represent different persons, gods, and animals while his music and dance change to show his progress in the spirit world and his different spiritual interactions. Many shamans practice ventriloquism and make use of their ability to accurately imitate the sounds of animals, nature, humans and other noises in order to provide the audience with the ambiance of the journey. Elaborate dances and recitations of songs and poetry are used to make the shamans spiritual adventures into a matter of living reality to his audience.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-150"><a href="#cite_note-150">[150]</a></sup></p>
<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Costume_and_accessories">Costume and accessories</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Shamanism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=35" title="Edit section: Costume and accessories">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h5>
<p>The shaman's attire varies throughout the region but his chief accessories are his coat, cap, and tambourine or drum. The transformation into an animal is an important aspect of the journey into the spirit world undertaken during shamanic rituals so the coat is often decorated with birds feathers and representations of animals, coloured handkerchiefs, bells and metal ornaments. The cap is usually made from the skin of a bird with the feathers and sometimes head, still attached.</p>
<p>The drum or tambourine is the essential means of communicating with spirits and enabling the shaman to reach altred states of consciousness on his journey. The drum, representing the universe in epitome, is often divided into equal halves to represent the earth and lower realms. Symbols and natural objects are added to the drum representing natural forces and heavenly bodies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-151"><a href="#cite_note-151">[151]</a></sup></p>
<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Shamanism_in_Tsarist_and_Soviet_Russia">Shamanism in Tsarist and Soviet Russia</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Shamanism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=36" title="Edit section: Shamanism in Tsarist and Soviet Russia">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h5>
<p>In Soviet Central Asia, the Soviet government persecuted and denounced shamans as practitioners of fraudulent medicine and perpetuators of outdated religious beliefs in the new age of science and logic. The radical transformations occurring after the October Socialist Revolution led to a sharp decrease in the activity of shamans. Shamans represented an important component in the traditional culture of Central Asians and because of their important role in society, Soviet organizations and campaigns targeted shamans in their attempt to eradicate traditional influences in the lives of the indigenous peoples. Along with persecution under the tsarist and Soviet regimes, the spread of Christianity and Islam had a role in the disintegration of native faith throughout central Asia. Poverty, political instability and foreign influence are also detrimental to a religion that requires publicity and patronage to flourish. By the 1980s most shamans were discredited in the eyes of their people by Soviet officials and physicians.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-152"><a href="#cite_note-152">[152]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_Asian_traditions">Other Asian traditions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Shamanism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=37" title="Edit section: Other Asian traditions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Wu_(shaman)" title="Wu (shaman)">Wu (shaman)</a></div>

<p>"<a href="/wiki/Jhakri" title="Jhakri">Jhakri</a>" is the common name used for shamans in <a href="/wiki/Sikkim" title="Sikkim">Sikkim</a>, <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">India</a>. They exist in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Limbu" title="Limbu">Limbu</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sunuwar" title="Sunuwar">Sunuwar</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rai_people" title="Rai people">Rai</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sherpa_people" title="Sherpa people">Sherpa</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kami" title="Kami">Kami</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tamang" title="Tamang">Tamang</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gurung" title="Gurung">Gurung</a> and <a href="/wiki/Lepcha_people" title="Lepcha people">Lepcha</a> communities.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-153"><a href="#cite_note-153">[153]</a></sup> They are inflluenced by <a href="/wiki/Hinduism" title="Hinduism">Hinduism</a>, Tibetan Buddhism, <a href="/wiki/Mun_(religion)" title="Mun (religion)">Mun</a> and Bön rites.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-154"><a href="#cite_note-154">[154]</a></sup></p>
<p>Shamanism is still widely practiced in the <a href="/wiki/Ryukyu_Islands" title="Ryukyu Islands">Ryukyu Islands</a> (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Okinawa" title="Okinawa">Okinawa</a>, <a href="/wiki/Japan" title="Japan">Japan</a>), where shamans are known as 'Noro' (all women) and 'Yuta'. 'Noro' generally administer public or communal ceremonies while 'Yuta' focus on civil and private matters. Shamanism is also practiced in a few rural areas in Japan proper. It is commonly believed that the <a href="/wiki/Shinto" title="Shinto">Shinto</a> religion is the result of the transformation of a shamanistic tradition into a religion. Forms of practice vary somewhat in the several Ryukyu islands, so that there is, for example, a distinct <b>Miyako</b> shamanism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-155"><a href="#cite_note-155">[155]</a></sup></p>
<p>Shamanism practices seem to have been preserved in the Catholic religious traditions of aborigines in <a href="/wiki/Taiwan" title="Taiwan">Taiwan</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-156"><a href="#cite_note-156">[156]</a></sup></p>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Vietnam" title="Vietnam">Vietnam</a>, shamans conduct rituals in many of the religious traditions that co-mingle in the majority and minority populations. In their rituals, music, dance, special garments and offerings are part of the performance that surround the spirit journey.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-157"><a href="#cite_note-157">[157]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Europe">Europe</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Shamanism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=38" title="Edit section: Europe">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Sami_shamanic_drum.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3264" data-file-width="2448" height="293" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ed/Sami_shamanic_drum.JPG/220px-Sami_shamanic_drum.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ed/Sami_shamanic_drum.JPG/330px-Sami_shamanic_drum.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ed/Sami_shamanic_drum.JPG/440px-Sami_shamanic_drum.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a>
